                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Dual-site+Substitution&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_dual-site%20substitution">dual-site substitution</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=O%E2%80%B23-na3ni2sbo6&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_O%E2%80%B23-Na3Ni2SbO6">O′3-Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Stacking+Disorder&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_stacking%20disorder">stacking disorder</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Cathode&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_cathode">cathode</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Due to the urgent demand for sustainable energy storage, lithium-ion batteries have achieved remarkable technical progress and great commercial success in the past decades. However, the rapidly rising price of the Li raw material has spurred the scientific community to search for cheaper alternatives, among which sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) seem to be the most promising choice due to its abundant natural resource. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1" aria-label="reference 1">(1)</a> A series of cathode materials for high-performance Na cells have been widely studied, such as layered oxides, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 2 and 3">(2,3)</a> polyanionic compounds, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> and Prussian blue analogues. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 6 and 7">(6,7)</a> According to Delmas et al., layered oxides with the general formula Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> (M is one or more transition metals) have two stacking arrangements that can be classified as P2 and O3 styles. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 8 and 9">(8,9)</a> The Na ions occupy prismatic sites for the P2 structure while those are located at octahedral sites in the O3 structure. Although the P2-type positive electrode materials present a high capacity versus a Na metal anode, its further application in full cells is restricted by three major disadvantages: the large volume change during cycles, the high hydroscopicity, and the low initial charge capacity unless additional sodium was compensated. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 10 and 11">(10,11)</a> In comparison, the layered O3-type compounds mentioned above without these drawbacks are likely to be more appropriate for practical sodium-ion batteries in future industrial manufacturing. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 12 and 13">(12,13)</a> Some start-up companies, in fact, have launched several commercial prototypes of Na-ion batteries with O3-type layered cathode materials. Faradion Limited, a UK-based company which was established in 2011, used Ni<sub>1–<i>x</i>–<i>y</i>–<i>z</i></sub>Mn<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mg<sub><i>y</i></sub>Ti<sub><i>z</i></sub>O<sub>2</sub> in their new prototypical pouch cells. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Previously, batteries based on O3–Na(Cu, Fe, Mn)O<sub>2</sub> electrode materials developed by Chinese HiNa Battery Technology were also reported. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">NaNiO<sub>2</sub>, one important and representative member with high theoretical capacity in the O3-type cathode family, has an acceptable electrochemical reversibility when the cutoff voltage is confined to 3.75 V, which is different from LiNiO<sub>2</sub> that is trapped in strong Li/Ni disorder. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 16 and 17">(16,17)</a> Unfortunately, a multiple phase transformation occurs in the charge–discharge process and leads to the irreversible phase of NiO with continuous capacity fade. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> To solve these problems, some transition-metal elements or electrochemically inactive elements, such as Co, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> Fe, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> Mn, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21,22)</a> Mg, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Bi, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24,25)</a> and Sb, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27 ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 26 to 28">(26−28)</a> have been introduced into the crystal structure as dopants. Interestingly, the displacement of one-third of Ni by high-valence cations (Sb<sup>5+</sup>) in monoclinic O′3-Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> could increase the cationic arrangement order to form a honeycomb-like superstructure TM layer where each SbO<sub>6</sub> octahedron is surrounded with six NiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. The increased average operation voltage compared with that of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> is attributed to the intense electrostatic repulsion between valence-lowered Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Sb<sup>5+</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Besides that, the substitution maintains the reversible specific capacity of NaNiO<sub>2</sub> (∼120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and the improved cycling structure stability. To mitigate the structure collapse and microcracking during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation that arises from the large volume variation, some divalent cations such as Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Zn<sup>2+</sup>, and Co<sup>2+</sup> were further used to substitute part of the remaining Ni<sup>2+</sup>, thereby forming Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2–x</sub>M<sub><i>x</i></sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> It is inevitable that this sacrifices the specific capacity if the ratio of active elements (Ni) is reduced. Moreover, the utilization of cobalt would add a huge cost for SIBs, though the rate performance is obviously promoted. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, considering one-third of the Na in Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> is inactive during charge/discharge, a novel dual-site substitution, where few Cs ions occupy Na sites and Mg ions take Ni sites, is performed to synthesize Na<sub>2.85</sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>, which retains a higher practical capacity. A transmission electron microscopy (TEM) analysis together with X-ray diffraction (XRD) Rietveld refinements are carried out to estimate the degree of stacking disorder only existing along the <i>c</i>-axis in different samples. The <i>in situ</i> and <i>ex situ</i> XRD characterizations give an all-around perspective to study how the stacking disorder associated with doping ions (Cs and Mg) affect the phase transition during electrochemical cycling, thereby increasing P′3–O′3 structure reversibility. A faster Na (de)intercalation kinetics is also confirmed by the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) and cyclic voltammetry (CV) at various scan rates. Although the positive effect of stacking disorder is difficult to distinguish from that of Cs and Mg ions, undoubtedly, this stacking disorder is one of the key factors in improving the electrochemical performance. As a result, the optimized Na<sub>2.85</sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> cathode delivers long cycling life, a high initial discharge capacity (120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>), and a considerable rate capability (66 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 C). Our work offers a new and effective tactic to improve the performance of a layered cathode for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Crystal Structure and Stacking Faults</h3><div class="NLM_p">The experimental samples of Na<sub>3-x</sub>Cs<sub><i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0, 0.03, 0.15, 0.27), Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>, and Na<sub>2.85</sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> were synthesized by a high-temperature solid-state reaction of stoichiometric precursors (more details are provided in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec4" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>). These samples are referred to as “NNSO”, “Cs0.03”, “Cs0.15”, “Cs0.27”, “Mg0.1”, and “Cs0.15Mg0.1” for short, respectively. The corresponding X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns, shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, are attributed to a monoclinic structure with <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> space group. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2a,b</a>, the Rietveld refinement results of NNSO, Cs0.15Mg0.1, Mg0.1, and Cs0.15 are illustrated. According to the specific refinement data in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Tables S1–S4</a>, the dual-site-substituted structure, where Cs ions with a larger ionic radius (1.67 Å) occupy Na sites and Mg ions take Ni sites (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e,f), present maximum lattice parameters and cell volume of <i>a</i> = 5.3096 Å, <i>b</i> = 9.1800 Å, <i>c</i> = 5.6381 Å, and <i>V</i> = 260.925 Å<sup>3</sup>. It is notable that the weakened XRD peaks at 2θ = 20–30° (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>), related to the original honeycomb order of Sb/Ni cations, may indicate that some stacking faults appear after introducing.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/medium/am2c15654_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. XRD patterns and Rietveld refinement results of (a) NNSO with <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> symmetry and Cs0.15Mg0.1 with (b) <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> symmetry, (c) <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> symmetry, and (d) <i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12 symmetry. Schematic diagrams of the crystal structures of (e) NNSO and (f) Cs0.15Mg0.1.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15654&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">As is known, only two space groups (<i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> and <i><i>P</i></i>3<sub>1</sub>12) are suitable for an O3-type layered structure with a honeycomb cation order. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> In order to obey a three-layer repeat period, only one kind of shift vector exists for the <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> symmetry and it is parallel to the <i>c</i> axis (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h). For the other symmetry (<i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12), the cyclical permutation of three shift vectors, which are related by a rotation of 120°, leads to a 3<sub>1</sub> screw axis vertical to the <i>a–b</i> plane (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>h). If the Ni/Sb order fully transforms to a random arrangement in a single crystallographic site, the structure should be described as <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> symmetry of α-NaFeO<sub>2</sub> and all peaks associated with the superstructure would disappear. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> To investigate the nature of the stacking faults caused by substitution, the <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> and <i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12 space groups were also used to refine the XRD data of Cs0.15Mg0.1. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c,d, the <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> structure symmetry gives an <i>R</i><sub>wp</sub> value of 11.78%, similar to that of the <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> model (11.09%), while an obviously inferior quality was obtained on fitting with the other honeycomb-ordered symmetry (<i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12). However, the XRD pattern of Cs0.15Mg0.1 still presents a very broad asymmetrical diffraction peak at 2θ = 19.3° ((020) plane, <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i>) which cannot be described in the <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> symmetry. Some conclusions can be inferred from these evidence. (1) These kind of stacking faults only occur along the <i>c</i> axis rather than in the <i>a–b</i> plane, where the honeycomb order is also well-developed. (2) Although the O3 stacking rule is still kept, the deviation of position for Ni/Sb atoms relative to those in the upper and next layers results in the appearance of Ni/Sb arranged disorder (schematic illustrations in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f,g). (3) The presence of the stacking disorder breaks the ideal monoclinic symmetry (<i>C</i>2/<i>m</i>) and raises the lattice to the <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> or <i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12 model to some degree. The real phase has an intermediate structure without full order/disorder among these three symmetries. Furthermore, for only Cs-doped samples, those five clear superstructural peaks (2θ = 20–30°) gradually transform into a single bulge peak upon an increase in Cs content, probably manifesting the enhancement of stacking disorder.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/medium/am2c15654_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) HR-TEM image and (b) SAED pattern of Cs0.15 along the [001] zone axis. (c) HR-TEM image and (d) SAED pattern of Cs0.15Mg0.1 along the [001]<sub>P</sub>/[001]<sub>R</sub> zone axis. (e) TEM image of Cs0.15Mg0.1 and corresponding EDS elemental mappings of Na, Cs, Ni, Mg, Sb, and O. Schematic diagrams of (f) the ordered structure and (g) the disordered structure. (h) Superimposed view of the three sets of layer positions for <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i>, <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i>, and <i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12 models, perpendicular to <i>a–b</i> plane. Red lines indicate the unit cells for these models.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15654&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">A transmission electron microscopy (TEM) analysis was performed to further test the crystallinity of NNSO, Cs0.15, and Cs0.15Mg0.1. The <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> symmetry in NNSO is confirmed by the clear lattice fringes with spacings (about 2.35 Å) of the (12<span class="overline">2</span>) plane in a high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) image (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>), as well as the diffraction spots in corresponding selected area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>) along the [011] zone axis. In <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c,d</a>, with the electron beam parallel to the [2–10] direction, the same space group can be verified again. For Cs0.15, viewed from either the [001] or [011] zone, HRTEM images exhibit fair lattice fringes of the (200) planes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a</a>). The corresponding SAED patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4b</a>) can be indexed by monoclinic symmetry (<i>C</i>2/<i>m</i>), though some rods of scattering reveal the existence of stacking disorder along <i>c</i> axis. In the HRTEM images of the Cs0.15Mg0.1 sample (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c), distinct spaced stripes can be attributed to the (130) plane of the <i>C</i>2/<i>m</i> superstructure. What is surprising is that some bright diffraction spots along the [001]<sub>P</sub>/[001]<sub>R</sub> zones can be indexed with the <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> space group, while the additional patterns can only be indexed as the other order symmetry (<i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12) (where the “R” and “P” subscripts denote the <i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> and <i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12 space groups, respectively). Combining the above discussions, the degree of stacking disorder of these three samples, NNSO &lt; Cs0.15 &lt; Cs0.15Mg0.1, can be reasonably deduced.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The X-ray energy dispersive spectrum (EDS) in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a> proves the existence of Na, Cs, Ni, Mg, Sb, and O elements in Cs0.15Mg0.1. The overlapping signals in elemental mapping display a uniform element distribution for both Cs0.15Mg0.1 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e) and Cs0.15 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4c</a>), which again verify the successful homogeneous doping of Cs and Mg. Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>) visually show the morphology of NNSO, Cs0.03, Cs0.15, Cs0.27, Mg0.1 and Cs0.15Mg0.1 powders. All six samples have similar platelike particles with sub-micrometer size, suggesting the more strongly preferred orientation of the (001) plane reflected in XRD patterns (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of NNSO, Cs0.03, Cs0.15, Cs0.27, Mg0.1, and Cs0.15Mg0.1 cathodes was investigated between 2 and 4 V in Na half-cells. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a–d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a,b</a> present the galvanostatic charge–discharge curves (GCD) profiles at a 0.1 C rate (10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) for different samples. There are two obvious plateaus in both charge (3.35, 3.67 V) and discharge (3.60, 3.21 V) curves of Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>. The Cs-only substitutions (Cs0.03, Cs0.15, Cs0.27) keep these main plateaus, as well as a steep rise between them. However, after introducing 5% of Mg (molar ratio, relative to Ni ions) into Cs0.15, the shorter plateau at about 3.60 V turns to a tender slope, indicating a transformation of a two-phase reaction to a solid-solution process. Although it has a theoretical capacity of 133 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (Ni<sup>2+</sup> to Ni<sup>3+</sup>), NNSO only delivers a first discharge capacity of 112 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, lower than those of Mg0.1, Cs0.03, and Cs0.15 samples, which results from inferior Na-ion diffusion kinetics. When the value of Cs doping is increased to 0.27, in contrast, the first discharge capacity is reduced to 109 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, suggesting that too many Cs ions doped in Na sites could restrain Na-ion migrations. The content of the electrochemically active element (Ni) slightly decreases in Cs0.15Mg0.1, whereas it still preserves a high discharge capacity of 120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. It is noteworthy that the dual-site-substituted sample has the least capacity loss after three cycles. This difference is due to the improved crystal structure stability during Na<sup>+</sup> extraction/insertion, and more details will be discussed below.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/medium/am2c15654_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of cathodes. Charge/discharge profiles of (a) NNSO, (b) Mg0.1, (c) Cs0.15, and (d) Cs0.15Mg0.1 between 2.0 and 4 V at a rate of 0.1 C (10 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). CV curves of (e) NNSO, (f) Mg0.1, (g) Cs0.15, and (h) Cs0.15Mg0.1 at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (i) Cycling performance of the four samples with Coulombic efficiency at a rate of 0.1 C. (j) Rate performance of the four samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15654&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cyclic voltammograms (CV) curves with three cycles for those six samples are displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e–h and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7c,d</a>. The pristine NNSO has two pairs of intense reduction/oxidation peaks, 3.17 V/3.49 V (O′3–P′3 phase transition) and 3.57 V/3.71 V (P′3–O1 phase transition). After three cycles, the increased polarizations casue all oxidation peaks to continuously shift to higher potential. Between these strong peaks, some small peaks (3.40, 3.46 V), originating from intermediate formation in the P′3 phase solid-solution process, can be observed and do not move in the next several cycles. Most of the above characteristics are maintained in the Cs-substituted samples, except that the minor peak around 3.22 V in the first cycle vanishes in Cs0.15 while it becomes stronger in Cs0.27. If the kinetic parameters become inferior during ion intercalation/deintercalation, according to previous studies, a new step may occur in charge–discharge curves accompanied by CV peak splits, especially under high current. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 35 to 37">(35−37)</a> Therefore, it is reasonable that this phenomenon can be attributed to an increased Na diffusion coefficient for the Cs0.15 sample and a decreased coefficient for the Cs0.27 sample in the phase transition of P′3 → O′3, as compared with that for NNSO. Only when Mg was introduced into TM sites, some small peaks (3.40, 3.46 V) in reduction reaction disappeared, embodying a more homogeneous P′3 solid-solution process together with TM layers smoothly gliding. The most improved diffusion kinetics, consequently, gives Cs0.15Mg0.1 both the highest reduction potentials and smallest polarizations (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h). In addition, the enhanced structure stability for this codoped sample is reflected by the roughly constant peak location in the next two CV cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">A comparison of the cycling performance of NNSO, Cs0.03, Cs0.15, Cs0.27, Mg0.1, and Cs0.15Mg0.1 cathodes at 0.1 C is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8a</a>, where Cs0.15Mg0.1 demonstrates the best cycling stability with a discharge capacity retention of 65.8% (79 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) after 100 cycles while the pristine sample (NNSO) only preserves 43% capacity. Even when the current density was increased to 2 C (200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>), the dual-site-substituted sample also exhibited an ameliorative cycling stability over 500 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9c</a>). It is noteworthy that a distinct gap of cycling stability emerges between NNSO and Cs0.15Mg0.1 at a high rate (2 C) but not at a small rate (0.1 C). This expansion effect probably originates from the difference in mechanical properties, which control mechanochemical degradation in the charge/discharge process, rather than the change in ion diffusion kinetics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> With regard to how the stacking disorder affects mechanical properties, it will be discussed in the next section. The rate capability of the six electrodes was also tested in a Na-ion half battery, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>j and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8b</a>. When the current density increases to 10 C, the other five samples except Cs0.15Mg0.1 are hardly electrochemically active, indicating an enlarged internal impedance caused by inferior Na-ion diffusion kinetics. The Cs0.15Mg0.1 electrode, with the best performance, presents discharge capacities of 118, 117, 115, 108, 91, and 66 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>j). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9a,b</a> shows the difference of charge–discharge curves at different rates for both NNSO and Cs0.15Mg0.1. In a word, the unusual dual-site-substitution tactic has improved the cycling stability and rate performance, whose values are comparable to those of other similar layered oxide cathodes for sodium-ion batteries reported previously (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S8</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">To study the variation of internal cell resistance, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was used for the NNSO and Cs0.15Mg0.1 electrodes before and after 50 cycles (2 C, 200 mA g<sup>–1</sup>). The Nyquist plots, circuit models for data fitting, and resulting resistance components are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S7</a>, where <i>R</i><sub>Ω</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>, and σ represent the Ohmic contact resistance, charge transfer resistance, solid electrolyte interphase resistance, and Warburg coefficient, respectively. The diffusion resistance <i>R</i><sub>ω</sub> can be calculated by the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a> (ω is the AC frequency)<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-5"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-6" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-7">R</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-8" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-9">ω</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-10"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-11" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-12"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-13" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-14"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-15">σ</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-16"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-17"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-18" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-19">ω</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-20" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-21">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-22">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-23">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>ω</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mi>σ</mi></mrow><mrow><msup><mrow><mi>ω</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p last">Therefore, the poor rate performance of NNSO, as compared with that of Cs0.15Mg0.1, can be attributed to a higher <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and diffusion resistance <i>R</i><sub>ω</sub>, among which the latter originates from slower sodium-ion diffusion kinetics in NNSO. After cycling, the <i>R</i><sub>Ω</sub> value of the NNSO electrode has a large increase of about 87% while that of Cs0.15Mg0.1 increased by 4.5%. Since the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> values are close for the two cycled electrodes, it is reasonable to ascribe the faster capacity fade to increased Ohmic contact resistance caused by crystal cracks for the NNSO electrode.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">2.3.  Phase Transition Mechanism during Electrochemical Cycling</h3><div class="NLM_p">To understand the structural change and reaction mechanism of these cathodes during Na<sup>+</sup> (de)intercalation, an <i>in situ</i> XRD analysis was performed in the first two electrochemical cycles. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a, for the pristine NNSO, the peaks related to the O′3 phase only display a weak shift until about 0.1 Na<sup>+</sup> is extracted from one formula unit and the voltage rapidly rises to the first plateau (3.36 V). After that, a series of new Bragg peaks corresponding to the P′3 phase (<i>C</i>2/<i>m</i>) emerge at 2θ = 15.78, 31.83, 35.29, and 36.50°. These diffraction peaks, in this two-phase reaction stage, gradually grow while others that belong to the coexisting O′3 phase become weak. At the end of the first plateau where the value of <i>y</i> in Na<sub>3–<i>y</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> is increased to close to 0.8, the O′3 phase is almost invisible, but the P′3 phase has a predominant role and presents a solid-solution behavior in the next step with (001) (15.78°) and (002) (31.83°) reflections moving to lower angles. From <i>y</i> = 1.2 to 1.6 in Na<sub>3–<i>y</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>, namely the second plateau (∼3.67 V), the appearance of a new set of Bragg peaks (2θ = 15.43, 31.17, 35.10, and 47.54°) indicates that a new phase is formed, identified as a layered O1 structure. After this narrower two-phase-reaction region, the dominant peaks of the O1 phase exhibit a slight shift together with a steep slope in the GCD curve until the voltage reaches 4 V. About 1.9 Na ions have been extracted per formula unit during the entire charge process, to result in the final O1 phase with the <i>P</i>3̅<sub>1</sub><i>m</i> space group.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/medium/am2c15654_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. <i>In situ</i> XRD patterns of (a) NNSO and (b) Cs0.15Mg0.1 at 0.1 C and their corresponding charge/discharge curves. Peaks marked with asterisks belong to Al foil and the Be window.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15654&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Upon discharge, the final O1 phase returns to the original O′3 phase, following the O1–P′3–O′3 multiple phase-transition steps inverse to that upon charge. Nevertheless, there is a small amount of residual P′3 phase existing in the electrode after two full cycles, revealing the incomplete reversibility of the O′3–P′3 phase transformation. After 100 cycles (0.1 C), the P′3 phase instead becomes the dominant component for NNSO, reflected by the <i>ex situ</i> XRD patterns in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a</a>. This partial irreversibility of the P′3 structure unambiguously accounts for the inferior cycle stability with 43% retention (after 100 cycles) of initial discharge capacity.</div><div class="NLM_p">For Cs0.15, the corresponding phase behavior appears to be very similar to that of NNSO, though there is some shift in peak location (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>). Nevertheless, at the end of the second cycle, the crystal structure fully converts back to its original O′3 phase without any visible change.</div><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, the <i>in situ</i> XRD patterns of Cs0.15Mg0.1 reveal some characteristics different from those of NNSO and Cs0.15. The most intuitive one is that P′3–O1 phase transition of this sample displays a quasi-solid-solution behavior rather than an explicit two-phase process. A comparison diagram with an enlarged view is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>. Some diffraction peaks related to the P′3 phase gradually move to lower angle until they can be indexed in the O1 phase, such as (001)<sub>P′3</sub> → (001)<sub>O1</sub>, (002)<sub>P′3</sub> → (002)<sub>O1</sub>, and (13<span class="overline">1</span>)<sub>P′3</sub> → (110)<sub>O1</sub>, while (130)<sub>P′3</sub> keeps its location before fading away. Second, some asymmetries are considerably mitigated in Cs0.15Mg0.1. For example, the difference (Δθ) of the (001)<sub>P′3</sub> position in the O′3–P′3 transition between charge and discharge almost vanishes, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>. Meanwhile, at the end of discharge, no visible signals of the P′3 phase can be seen.</div><div class="NLM_p">The changes in interlayer distance and crystal volume for NNSO and Cs0.15Mg0.1 in the charge/discharge process are calculated from the <i>in situ</i> XRD patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a,b). Upon Na-ion extraction/insertion, the interlayer distance of NNSO undergoes two abrupt conversions in the biphasic regions of O′3–P′3 and P′3–O1, and its bulk crystal volume suffer from sharp variations with a maximum change of ∼7 Å<sup>3</sup>. In contrast, there are smaller changes in interlayer distance and cell volume in the O′3–P′3 transition process of the codoped sample, a more continuous variation of these parameters also being displayed in the P′3–O1 biphasic region. The entire volumetric change in a full electrochemical cycle is reduced to ∼4 Å<sup>3</sup>. All of these beneficial characteristics are attributed to the improved mechanical properties, including internal stresses and particle toughness, which would partly determine the degradation of sodium storage capacity over long-term cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/medium/am2c15654_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Evolutions of interlayer distance (<i>c</i>* sin β), crystal volume, and charge/discharge curves as a function of Na<sup>+</sup> content for (a) NNSO and (b) Cs0.15Mg0.1, calculated from the <i>in situ</i> XRD patterns.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15654&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">What can be seen in SEM pictures of the three aging electrodes (100 cycles, 0.1 C) (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12b,d,f</a>) is that more fragments and bigger particles are stripped from bulk crystals and attach to the surface of the NNSO sample, compared with the other two (Cs0.15, Cs0.15Mg0.1). There are only slight diffraction signals of the P′3 structure emerging in XRD patterns of aging electrodes for Cs0.15 and Cs0.15Mg0.1 (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12c,e</a>), while the P′3 phase becomes dominant part for NNSO after cycling (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12a</a>). Beyond that, the relative diffraction intensity of O′3 phase in the Cs0.15 electrode after long-term cycling hugely weakens in comparison with the peaks of Al foils (noted as “*”), more serious in the NNSO electrode. No same trends for Cs0.15Mg0.1 probably indicate that the amorphization of layered oxides during electrochemical phase-transition is inhibited by the dual-site substitution. Based on above discussions, it is quite clear that the improved mechanical properties in Cs0.15Mg0.1 remarkably enhance the phase reversibility and cycling stability through decreasing cell resistance by suppressing crystal fractures and dimensional changes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In fact, the ordered or disordered arrangement of TM atoms in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TMO<sub>2</sub> would cause a different electrochemical performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38 ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 38 to 41">(38−41)</a> As reported, the full disorder of Cr and Ti in P2–Na<sub>0.6</sub>Cr<sub>0.6</sub>Ti<sub>0.4</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is responsible for the prominent rate capability and long cycle life. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The ordered distribution of cations in P3–Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> leads to structure stress and capacity fade companied with different dimensional changes in TMO<sub>6</sub> units, while the corresponding cation-disordered P3–Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> presents a better cycling performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 41 and 42">(41,42)</a> Therefore, this improved cycling stability of Cs0.15Mg0.1 could also be ascribed to incomplete disorder, though a quantitative relationship between them has yet to be studied further.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">2.4.  Na Ion Diffusion Kinetics</h3><div class="NLM_p">The galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> was also employed to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics in NNSO, Mg0.1, Cs0.15, and Cs0.15Mg0.1 cathodes. One single period of GITT measurement contains a constant current pulse (<i>I</i>) and an equilibration process (zero current). As exhibited in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10b</a>, the voltage of a galvanic cell, <i>E</i>, is recorded as a function of time. If the voltage <i>E</i> is a linear function of <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-24"><span class="MJXp-msqrt" id="MJXp-Span-25"><span class="MJXp-surd"><span style="font-size: 134%; margin-top: 0.104em;">√</span></span><span class="MJXp-root"><span class="MJXp-rule" style="border-top: 0.08em solid;"></span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-26">t</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="inline"><msqrt><mi>t</mi></msqrt></math></script> over the time of constant current pulse, the Na-ion diffusion coefficient, <i>D</i>, can be calculated from the simplified <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-27"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-28">D</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-29" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-30" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-31"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-32">4</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-33"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-34">π</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-35">τ</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-36"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-37" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-38"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-39" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-40" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-41"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-42"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-43" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-44">m</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-45" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-46">B</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-47"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-48" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-49">V</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-50" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-51">M</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-52"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-53"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-54" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-55">M</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-56" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-57">B</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-58">A</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-59" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-60" style="vertical-align: 1.54em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-61">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-62"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-63" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-64"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-65" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-66" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-67"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-68">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-69"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-70" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-71">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-72" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-73">S</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-74"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-75">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-76"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-77" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-78">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-79" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-80">τ</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-81" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-82" style="vertical-align: 1.54em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-83">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-84" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 1em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-85">τ</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-86" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">≪</span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-87" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-88"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-89"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-90" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-91">L</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-92" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-93">2</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-94"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-95">D</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left" indentshift="3.0em" indentshiftfirst="0em" linebreak="auto"><mi>D</mi><mo>=</mo><mfrac><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow><mrow><mi>π</mi><mi>τ</mi></mrow></mfrac><msup><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>m</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi>V</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">M</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>M</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi></mrow></msub><mi>A</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>τ</mi></mrow></msub></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mo linebreak="goodbreak" lspace="0em" rspace="1em">⁣</mo><mi>τ</mi><mo>≪</mo><mfrac><mrow><msup><mrow><mi>L</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mrow><mrow><mi>D</mi></mrow></mfrac></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>Here <i>m</i><sub>B</sub>, <i>M</i><sub>B</sub>, and <i>V</i><sub>M</sub> represent the actual weight, molar weight, and molar volume of electrode materials, respectively. <i>A</i> is the contact area for the electrolyte and electrode. Δ<i>E</i><sub>S</sub>, Δ<i>E</i><sub>τ</sub>, and τ are the change of steady-state voltage, the voltage variation during one constant current pulse, and the pulse duration time, respectively.</div><div class="NLM_p">The GITT profiles (<i>E</i> vs state of charge) are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a,b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13a,b</a>, and all of them display two charge/discharge plateaus associated with two biphasic reactions (O′3–P′3, P′3–O1), which are in line with GCD, CV, and <i>in situ</i> XRD measurements. Among these four samples, Cs0.15Mg0.1 shows the lowest voltage polarization, namely the deviation of equilibrium voltage between charge and discharge processes, which may suggest a faster Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13c</a>). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c,d, the calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient <i>D</i> is plotted as a function of stoichiometric parameter <i>y</i> in Na<sub>3–<i>y</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>, Na<sub>3–<i>y</i></sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>, Na<sub>2.85-<i>y</i></sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>, and Na<sub>2.85-<i>y</i></sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>. The <i>D</i> values of all four samples reveal the same trend in the whole electrochemical process and increase to a high level (10<sup>–11</sup>–10<sup>–10</sup> m<sup>–2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) in the P′3–O1 biphasic region when <i>y</i> &gt; 1.4. More importantly, the Cs0.15Mg0.1 cathode has values (10<sup>–14</sup>–10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>–2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>) about 1 order of magnitude higher than those of the NNSO cathode (10<sup>–15</sup>–10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>–2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>), which is responsible for its high-rate performance. In consideration of some possible deviations from the GITT method, cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements with different scan rates were conducted for Cs0.15Mg0.1 and NNSO cathodes to again verify this conclusion. The CV curves and the linear relationship between the peak currents <i>I</i><sub>Peak</sub> and the square root of scan rates <i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>. The diffusion coefficient in the Cs0.15Mg0.1 cathode is 4–7 times higher than in NNSO, in either the peak 1 (O′3–P′3 transition) or the peak 2 (P′3–O1) region. Obviously, this result is basically consistent with the trend in GITT measurements. The disorder characteristic of TM atoms, induced by the unusual dual-site doping, is likely to determine Na-ion diffusion kinetics through affecting the coordination environment of Na ions.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/medium/am2c15654_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. GITT curves of (a) NNSO and (b) Cs0.15Mg0.1. Calculated Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients of NNSO, Mg0.1, Cs0.15, Cs0.15Mg0.1 on (c) the charge and (d) the discharge process.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/asset/images/large/am2c15654_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c15654&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">3.  Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we have successfully adjusted the degree of stacking disorder along the <i>c</i>-axis in honeycomb-like Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> by using different doping strategies. With an unusual dual-site substitution, the synthesized Na<sub>2.85</sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> possesses so much stacking disorder that its lattice is raised to higher symmetries (<i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i> and <i>P</i>3<sub>1</sub>12) in some ways. Due to small amounts of doped elements and fast ion diffusion kinetics, a high practical discharge capacity (120 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>) and considerable rate performance (66 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 C) are exhibited for the Na<sub>2.85</sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> cathode. A gentler structure evolution and smaller volume change caused by improved mechanical properties, upon Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation, enable Na<sub>2.85</sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> to have a longer cycling life, compared with the pristine Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>. There is no doubt that the stacking disorder has a positive influence on upgrading electrochemical performance, in spite of the fact that the same effects originating from doping ions (Cs and Mg) cannot be excluded or quantified. Our work rolls out a novel tactic to regulate O3-type layered Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MO<sub>2</sub> materials without sacrificing capacity, which could be beneficial for developing other high-performance electrode materials for practical SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">4.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">4.1.  Material Synthesis</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The Na<sub>3-x</sub>Cs<sub><i>x</i></sub>Ni<sub>2</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.03, 0.15, 0.27), Na<sub>3</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub>, and Na<sub>2.85</sub>Cs<sub>0.15</sub>Ni<sub>1.9</sub>Mg<sub>0.1</sub>SbO<sub>6</sub> samples were synthesized via a conventional high-temperature solid-state method. Stoichiometric amounts of anhydrous Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (10% molar excess of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> was added), Cs<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, NiO, MgO, and Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> were ball-milled with acetone for 6 h at 400 r min<sup>–1</sup>. Subsequently, the mixture was dried for hours at 70 °C. Finally, the powders were pressed into pellets and calcined in a furnace at 950 °C for 12 h in an air atmosphere, followed by regrinding and reheating at the same temperature for 6 h. The temperature ramp rate in every process was 3 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. After they were cooled to room temperature, the light green products were immediately transferred into an argon-filled glovebox.</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">4.2.  Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">A D8 ADVANCE X-ray diffractometer (XRD) with Cu Kα radiation (λ = 1.5418 Å) was used to measure powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) data. Le Bail and Rietveld refinements were carried out using the General Structure Analysis System (GSAS) program. Crystal structures were drawn with VESTA. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images were collected with a field-emission scanning electron microscope (Zeiss Sigma 300). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) measurements, selected-area electron diffraction (SAED), and elemental mapping were performed on a FEI Tecnai G2 F20 electron microscope coupled with energy-dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS). For the <i>in situ</i> XRD measurements, special dummy cells were assembled in an aluminum housing equipped with a beryllium window.</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i15" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i15">4.3.  Electrochemical Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">To study the electrochemical properties, the as-synthesized powders, Super P, and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) binder were mixed in <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) with a mass ratio of 7/2/1. Before being dried in a vacuum oven at 90 °C for 12 h, the homogeneous slurry was cast onto Al foils by using a doctor blade. After that, the dried electrodes were punched into circular disks with a diameter of 11 mm, and the active material loading was about 2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. In an argon-filled glovebox with H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> contents lower than 0.1 ppm, the prepared electrodes were paired with Na metal (anode) in R2032 coin-type cells to form Na half-cells. Glass fiber microporous membranes (Whatman) and a 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> solution in a mixture of anhydrous ethylene carbonate (EC)/dimethyl carbonate (DMC) (1/1 by volume) were used in all cells as the separators and the electrolyte, respectively. A LAND battery test system (Wuhan Kingnuo Electronic Co., Ltd. China) was used to determine the electrochemical performance (GCD, GITT) at 25 °C. For GITT measurements, the cells underwent a 30 min charging/discharging process at 0.1 C (mA g<sup>–1</sup>) and 10 h relaxation. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) and EIS tests were conducted on an AUTOLAB instrument (PGSTAT302N).</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i16">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c15654?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c15654</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline"><i>Ex situ</i> XRD patterns, HR-TEM images, SAED patterns, charge/discharge profiles, X-ray energy dispersive spectrum, SEM images, CV curves, <i>in situ</i> XRD patterns, GITT curves, EIS plots, and Rietveld refinement results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c15654/suppl_file/am2c15654_si_001.pdf">am2c15654_si_001.pdf (2.54 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c15654" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C9CS00846B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P. Soc. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>49</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2342</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">2377</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin Ting; Li Huangxu; Zhu Kunjie; Wang Peng-Fei; Liu Pei; Jiao Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">49</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2342-2377</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising candidates for smart grids and large-scale energy storage systems (EESs) due to their significant benefits of abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  Among the previously reported cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition-metal oxides and polyanion-type materials are considered to be the most attractive options.  Although many layered transition-metal oxides can provide high capacity due to their small molecular weight, their further application is hindered by low output voltage (mostly lower than 3.5 V), irreversible phase transition as well as storage instability.  Comparatively, polyanion-type materials exhibit higher operating potentials due to the inductive effect of polyanion groups.  Their robust 3D framework significantly decreases the structural variations during sodium ion de/intercalation.  Moreover, the effect of strong X-O (X = S, P, Si, etc.) covalent bonds can effectively inhibit oxygen evolution.  These advantages contribute to the superior cycle stability and high safety of polyanion-type materials.  However, low electronic conductivity and limited capacity still restrict their further application.  This review summarizes the recent progress of polyanion-type materials for SIBs, which include phosphates, fluorophosphates, pyrophosphates, mixed phosphates, sulfates, and silicates.  We also discuss the remaining challenges and corresponding strategies for polyanion-type materials.  We hope this review can provide some insights into the development of polyanionic materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-019-08359-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mariyappan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rousse, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jacquet, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deschamps, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">David, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mirvaux, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Freeland, J.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Higher energy and safer sodium ion batteries via an electrochemically made disordered Na3V2(PO4)2F3 material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yan, Guochun; Mariyappan, Sathiya; Rousse, Gwenaelle; Jacquet, Quentin; Deschamps, Michael; David, Renald; Mirvaux, Boris; Freeland, John William; Tarascon, Jean-Marie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">585</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Research</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The growing need to store an increasing amt. of renewable energy in a sustainable way has rekindled interest for sodium-ion battery technol., owing to the natural abundance of sodium.  Presently, sodium-ion batteries based on Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C are the subject of intense research focused on improving the energy d. by harnessing the third sodium, which has so far been reported to be electrochem. inaccessible.  Here, we are able to trigger the activity of the third sodium electrochem. via the formation of a disordered NaxV2(PO4)2F3 phase of tetragonal symmetry (I4/mmm space group).  This phase can reversibly uptake 3 sodium ions per formula unit over the 1 to 4.8 V voltage range, with the last one being re-inserted at 1.6 V vs Na+/Na0.  We track the sodium-driven structural/charge compensation mechanism assocd. to the new phase and find that it remains disordered on cycling while its av. vanadium oxidn. state varies from 3 to 4.5.  Full sodium-ion cells based on this phase as pos. electrode and carbon as neg. electrode show a 10-20% increase in the overall energy d.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1002/ange.201206854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goodenough, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the unprecedentedly increasing demand for renewable and clean energy sources, the sodium-ion battery (SIB) is emerging as an alternative or complementary energy storage candidate to the present com. lithium-ion battery due to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources.  Layered transition metal oxides and Prussian blue analogs are reviewed in terms of their com. potential as cathode materials for SIBs.  The recent progress in research on their half cells and full cells for the ultimate application in SIBs are summarized.  In addn., their electrochem. performance, suitability for scaling up, cost, and environmental concerns are compared in detail with a brief outlook on future prospects.  It is anticipated that this review will inspire further development of layered transition metal oxides and Prussian blue analogs for SIBs, esp. for their emerging commercialization.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1016/0167-2738(81)90076-X"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Braconnier, J.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fouassier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hagenmuller, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Intercalation of Sodium in Na<sub>x</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> Bronzes</span>. <i>Solid State Ionics</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1981</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">165</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">169</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical intercalation of sodium in sodium cobalt oxide (NaxCoO2) bronzes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Delmas, Claude; Braconnier, Jean Jacques; Fouassier, Claude; Hagenmuller, Paul</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Ionics</span>
