    </div><div class="casAbstract">The chem. study of the NaxCoO2 system (0.5 ≤ x ≤1) has shown previously the existence of bronze-type phases with layer structure.  In each material, the lattice is built up by sheets of edge-sharing CoO6 octahedra allowing Na+ to be intercalated with trigonal prismatic surrounding (AABBCC or AABB packing of O) for small values of x and octahedral environment (ABCABC packing of O) for larger ones.  Discharge potentials (2.0 &lt; V &lt; 3.5 V) were measured as a function of the compn.  The electrochem. intercalation of Na in all these materials is reversible within their existence range.  During the electrochem. intercalation, a reversible charge is obsd. between phases having AABBCC and ABCABC packing of O.  This behavior can be explained by the relatively small displacement of the sheets.  In contrast, for the material with AABB packing of O, no transition occurs because a packing change requires breaking of Co-O bonds, which is apparently impossible at room temp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1038/nmat2920"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Berthelot, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carlier, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Investigation of the P2-Na<sub>x</sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> Phase Diagram</span>. <i>Nat.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na layered oxides NaxCoO2 form one of the most fascinating low-dimensional and strongly correlated systems; in particular P2-NaxCoO2 exhibits various single-phase domains with different Na+/vacancy patterns depending on the Na concn.  Here the authors used Na batteries to clearly depict the P2-NaxCoO2 phase diagram for x ≥ 0.50.  By coupling the electrochem. process with an in situ x-ray diffraction expt., the authors identified the succession of single-phase or two-phase domains appearing on Na intercalation with a rather good accuracy compared with previous studies.  The authors reported new single-phase domains and the authors underlined the thermal instability of some ordered phases from an electrochem. study at various temps.  As each phase was characterized by the position of its Fermi level vs. the Na+/Na couple, the synthesis of each material, even in large amts., can be carried out electrochem.  The phys. properties of the as-prepd. Na1/2CoO2 and Na2/3CoO2 ordered phases were characterized and compared.  Electrochem. processes are an accurate route to precisely study in a continuous way such a complex system and provide a new way to synthesize materials with a very narrow existence range.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref10" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1021/cm403855t"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, D.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Identifying the Critical Role of Li Substitution in P2-Nax[LiyNizMn1-y-z]O2 (0 &lt; x, y, z &lt; 1) Intercalation Cathode Materials for High-Energy Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Jing; Lee, Dae Hoe; Clement, Raphaele J.; Yu, Xiqian; Leskes, Michal; Pell, Andrew J.; Pintacuda, Guido; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Grey, Clare P.; Meng, Ying Shirley</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-substituted layered P2-Na0.80[Li0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66]O2 is studied as an advanced cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  Both n diffraction and NMR spectroscopy are used to elucidate the local structure, and they reveal that most of the Li ions are located in transition metal (TM) sites, preferably surrounded by Mn ions.  To characterize structural changes occurring upon electrochem. cycling, in situ synchrotron x-ray diffraction is conducted.  No significant phase transformation is obsd. up to 4.4 V charge for this material, unlike Li-free P2-type Na cathodes.  The presence of monovalent Li ions in the TM layers allows more Na ions to reside in the prismatic sites, stabilizing the overall charge balance of the compd.  Consequently, more Na ions remain in the compd. upon charge, the P2 structure is retained in the high voltage region, and the phase transformation is delayed.  Ex situ NMR is conducted on samples at different states of charge/discharge to track Li-ion site occupation changes.  Surprisingly, Li is mobile, some Li ions migrate from the TM layer to the Na layer at high voltage, and yet this process is highly reversible.  Novel design principles for Na cathode materials are proposed from an atomistic level understanding of the underlying electrochem. processes.  These principles enable one to devise an optimized, high capacity, and structurally stable compd. as a potential cathode material for high-energy Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref11" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA09833F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jo, C.                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Controllable charge capacity using a black additive for high-energy-density sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jo, Chang-Heum; Choi, Ji Ung; Yashiro, Hitoshi; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-deficient P2 or P'2 type layered materials are known to deliver high capacity with acceptable capacity retention.  However, the initial charge capacity is substantially lower than the discharge capacity because of the insufficient amt. of sodium in their crystal structure, hindering practical application of these materials as cathodes in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).  This limitation can be overcome by introducing a sacrificial salt additive, which participates in the electrochem. oxidn. reaction by releasing enough sodium ions to compensate for the insufficient sodium content in the cathode material.  Herein, the sacrificial salt NaNO2 was blended with a high-capacity orthorhombic P'2 type Na2/3[Co0.05Mn0.95]O2 cathode material, increasing the initial charge capacity from 154-210 mA h g-1.  During electrochem. oxidn., the NaNO2 was oxidatively decompd. by the following reaction: NaNO2 → NO2 + Na+ + e-, where NO2 is an oxidizer that enables full desodiation to Na0[Co0.05Mn0.95]O2.  The first coulombic efficiency of Na2/3[Co0.05Mn0.95]O2 was improved from 1.38-0.98 by virtue of the sacrificing and oxidizing roles of NaNO2.  These findings demonstrate that the introduction of NaNO2 as an addnl. sodium source in cathodes can open new opportunities for the adoption of sodium-deficient cathode materials in practical SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref12" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201411788"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishida, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Layered P2- and O3-Type Composite as a High-Energy Cathode for Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Angew.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. and structural evolution of monoclinic NaNiO2 as a cathode material for Na-ion battery is reported.  The initial charge capacity reached 160 mA h g-1 and the following discharge capacity of 114.6 mA h g-1, within the voltage range of 4.0-1.5 V at C/10.  The multiple phase transition leading to O'3, P'3, P''3, O''3, and O'''3 stacking types (NaNiO2, Na0.91NiO2, Na0.84NiO2, Na0.81NiO2 and Na0.79NiO2 transitions, resp., according to a previous report) during the 1st charge/discharge process is analyzed using ex situ and in situ XRD techniques, and the stoichiometry of each phase is herein revised.  The charge/discharge profile shows a highly reversible nature of the cathode, except that fully sodiated phase could not be achieved at the subsequent discharge.  Two new phases were discovered: a monoclinic O3 structure (designated as O''''3) at the beginning of the charge (and end of discharge) and a P3 structure (designated as P'''3) at 3.38 V that appeared only during the charge process.  The compn. of the new O''''3-phase corresponds to Na0.83NiO2, which is the closest to the fully sodiated phase at room temp. achieved during the discharge process reported up to date, and the compn. of the new P'''3-phase corresponds approx. to Na0.50NiO2.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1149/2.023302jes"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vassilaras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ceder, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Properties of Monoclinic NaNiO<sub>2</sub></span>. <i>J. Synthesized in the O3-type layered structure, both materials show reasonable electrochem. activities at room temp., delivering approx. 0.5 Na per formula unit at C/10 discharge.  More Na can be reversibly cycled in NaNi0.5Co0.5O2 at elevated temp. and/or in an extended voltage window, while NaNi0.5Fe0.5O2 shows significant capacity fading at a high voltage cutoff which is likely due to Fe4+ migration.  In situ X-ray diffraction shows that the structural changes in the two materials upon desodiation are very different.  NaNi0.5Co0.5O2 goes through many different two-phase reactions including three different O3-type and three different P3-type structures during cycling, producing a voltage profile with multiple plateau-like features.  In contrast, NaNi0.5Fe0.5O2 has a smooth voltage profile and shows the typical O3-P3 phase transition without lattice distortion seen in other materials.  This different structural evolution upon desodiation and re-sodiation can be explained by the electronic structure of the mixed transition metals and how it perturbs the ordering between Na ions differently.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref19" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.6b02440"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bo, S.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Jahn-Teller Assisted Na Diffusion for High Performance Na Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Xin; Wang, Yan; Wu, Di; Liu, Lei; Bo, Shou-Hang; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na energy storage technol. is strategically attractive for large scale applications such as grid energy storage.  We show in this paper that there is a clear relation between the Jahn-Teller activity of a transition metal ion at the end of charge and the mobility of Na in a cathode material.  This is particularly important as mobility at the end of charge limits the capacity of current materials.  Hence, by using this classical piece of physics in the battery world, it is possible to create higher capacity Na-cathode materials.  Even more exciting is that the ideal element to impart this effect on cathodes is Fe, which is the least expensive of the transition metal oxides and can therefore enable low cost cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref20" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.11.015"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Vassilaras, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Toumar, A. In the narrower potential range of 2.5-4.3 V the material exhibits high average discharge potential (3.4 V versus Na/Na(+)), exceptional average coulombic efficiencies (&gt;99.9%), and extraordinary capacity retention (90.2% after 601 cycles).         </div><div class="casAbstract">O3-type layered sodium transition metal oxides, for example, NaNi0.5Mn0.5-zTizO2, having one sodium per transition metal ion could be attractive pos. electrode materials for achieving high energy d. sodium-ion batteries, provided that we can reversibly utilize their full Na content.  However, the layered structure on cycling undergoes a series of phase transitions in which the fully desodiated O1 phase shows a huge redn. in cell vol. together with cation migration, both of which are detrimental for long-term cycling performance.  Hence, the practical capacity of layered oxides is restricted to solely ∼0.5-0.6 Na (oxidn. up to ∼4 V vs Na+/Na0), avoiding the complete removal of sodium.  Herein, we show that the partial substitution of a redox-active Ni2+ cation by an inactive one (e.g., Zn2+ to form NaNi0.45Zn0.05Mn0.4Ti0.1O2) suppresses the phase transitions at high voltage (&gt;4 V vs Na+/Na0) and helps in utilizing the max. capacity of the material (170 mAh g-1 with ∼0.8 Na) without much degrdns. upon long cycling.  The fully charged phase (Na0.2Ni0.45Zn0.05Mn0.4Ti0.1O2), as detd. by high-resoln. electron transmission microscopy, shows a P3-O1 intergrowth structure in which the O1 phase is present only locally as nanoscale domains.  We believe that the formation of P3-O1 intergrowths in the Zn-substituted material, in contrast to the distinct O1 phase for unsubstituted NaNi0.5Mn0.4Ti0.1O2, restricts structural degrdns. during cycling and improves the long-term cycling stability.  Similar substitution chem. can be extended to Cu2+ and Mg2+ ions as well.  The NaNi0.45Zn0.05Mn0.4Ti0.1O2 pos. electrode material on implementation in 18650 Na-ion cells show electrochem. performances comparable to that of polyanionic Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C cells.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA08661F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bhange, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The need to find Na ion battery (SIB) cathodes with high voltage, capacity and improved cycle life has stimulated research on Na contg. layered transition metal oxides.  With this perspective, the electrochem. properties of highly ordered, honeycomb layered Na3Ni2BiO6 with a monoclinic superstructure are explored as a cathode material in SIBs.  It has been demonstrated that Na3Ni2BiO6 delivers a discharge capacity of 106 mAh/g, having high voltage plateaus at 3.50 and 3.25 V, with marginal capacity fading after 50 cycles.  Operando x-ray diffraction studies during charging/discharging reveal 2 reversible 2-phase transition mechanisms (initial O3 phase → P3 intermediate phase → O1 final phase) during sodium extn.  Ex situ x-ray absorption spectroscopy reveals the charge compensation mechanism for the reversible Ni3+/Ni2+ as an active redox couple while Bi5+ being inactive during cycling.  Extended x-ray absorption fine structure anal. shows highly reversible local structural changes around both Ni and Bi atoms occurring during electrochem. cycling.  Unique local structure changes esp. around Ni atoms due to the honeycomb ordering and size mismatch between Ni2+ and Bi5+ are revealed by EXAFS anal. during charging and discharging, which is quite different from the local structure changes in regular layer structured NaMO2 (M =transition metals) cathode materials.  These results suggest that honeycomb layered metal oxides with the general formula, Na3M(II)2M(V)O6, can be considered as candidates for high voltage and long life cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.7b00930"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-based layered oxides are among the leading cathode candidates for Na-ion batteries, toward potential grid energy storage, having large specific capacity, good ionic cond., and feasible synthesis.  Despite their excellent prospects, the performance of layered intercalation materials is affected by both a phase transition induced by the gliding of the transition metal slabs and air-exposure degrdn. within the Na layers.  Here, this problem is significantly mitigated by selecting 2 ions with very different M-O bond energies to construct a highly ordered Ni6-ring superstructure within the transition metal layers in a model compd. (NaNi2/3Sb1/3O2).  By virtue of substitution of 1/3 Ni with Sb in NaNiO2, the existence of these ordered Ni6-rings with super-exchange interaction to form a sym. at. configuration and degenerate electronic orbital in layered oxides can not only largely enhance their air stability and thermal stability, but also increase the redox potential and simplify the phase-transition process during battery cycling.  The findings reveal that the ordered Ni6-ring superstructure is beneficial for constructing highly stable layered cathodes and calls for new paradigms for better design of layered materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201601698"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The effect of Zn2+ substitution on the electrochem. performance and the structural evolution upon cycling of Na3Ni2SbO6 and Na3Zn0.5Ni1.5SbO6 is reported.  Samples were synthesized by solid state route and characterised by ex-situ X-ray diffraction and ex-situ23Na solid state NMR, the results show that the partial substitution of Ni2+ by Zn2+ alters the superstructure symmetry and leads to a disordered lattice.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, the rational design of O'3-type Na[Ni2/3-xCoxSb1/3]O2, a solid soln. of Na[Ni2/3Sb1/3]O2-Na[Co2/3Sb1/3]O2, is introduced.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ordered and Disordered Polymorphs of Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2: Honeycomb-Ordered Cathodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ma, Jeffrey; Bo, Shou-Hang; Wu, Lijun; Zhu, Yimei; Grey, Clare P.; Khalifah, Peter G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">27</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2387-2399</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are appealing alternatives to Li-ion battery systems for large-scale energy storage applications in which elemental cost and abundance are important.  Although it is difficult to find Na-ion batteries which achieve substantial specific capacities at voltages above 3 V (vs Na+/Na), the honeycomb-layered compd. Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2 can deliver up to 130 mAh/g of capacity at voltages above 3 V with this capacity concd. in plateaus at 3.27 and 3.64 V.  Comprehensive crystallog. studies have been carried out in order to understand the role of disorder in this system which can be prepd. in both "disordered" and "ordered" forms, depending on the synthesis conditions.  The av. structure of Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2 is always found to adopt an O3-type stacking sequence, though different structures for the disordered (R‾3m, #166, a = b = 3.06253(3) Å and c = 16.05192(7) Å) and ordered variants (C2/m, #12, a = 5.30458(1) Å, b = 9.18432(1) Å, c = 5.62742(1) Å and β = 108.2797(2)°) are demonstrated through the combined Rietveld refinement of synchrotron X-ray and time-of-flight neutron powder diffraction data.  However, pair distribution function studies find that the local structure of disordered Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2 is more correctly described using the honeycomb-ordered structural model, and solid-state NMR studies confirm that the well-developed honeycomb ordering of Ni and Sb cations within the transition-metal layers is indistinguishable from that of the ordered phase.  The disorder is instead found to mainly occur perpendicular to the honeycomb layers with an obsd. coherence length of not much more than 1 nm seen in electron diffraction studies.  When the Na environment is probed through 23Na solid-state NMR, no evidence is found for prismatic Na environments, and a bulk diffraction anal. finds no evidence of conventional stacking faults.  The lack of long-range coherence is instead attributed to disorder among the three possible choices for distributing Ni and Sb cations into a honeycomb lattice in each transition-metal layer, disrupting the Li2MnO3-type stacking of the honeycomb layers preferred in the ordered form of Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2.  It is obsd. that the full theor. discharge capacity expected for a Ni3+/2+ redox couple (133 mAh/g) can be achieved for the ordered variant but not for the disordered variant (∼110 mAh/g).  The first 3.27 V plateau during charging is found to be assocd. with a two-phase O3-P3 structural transition, with the P3 stacking sequence persisting throughout all further stages of desodiation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1021/acs.inorgchem.6b01078"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bak, S.                         cas_cit34R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">34</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Quantification of Honeycomb Number-Type Stacking Faults: Application to Na3Ni2BiO6 Cathodes for Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Jue; Yin, Liang; Wu, Lijun; Bai, Jianming; Bak, Seong-Min; Yu, Xiqian; Zhu, Yimei; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Khalifah, Peter G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Inorganic Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">55</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">17</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">8478-8492</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Ordered and disordered samples of honeycomb-lattice Na3Ni2BiO6 were investigated as cathodes for Na-ion batteries and it was detd. that the ordered sample exhibits better electrochem. performance with a specific capacity of 104 mA h/g delivered at plateaus of 3.5 and 3.2 V (vs Na+/Na) with minimal capacity fade during extended cycling.  Advanced imaging and diffraction investigations showed that the primary difference between ordered and disordered samples is the amt. of no.-type stacking faults assocd. with the three possible centering choices for each honeycomb layer.  A labeling scheme for assigning the no. position of honeycomb layers is described, and it is shown that the translational shift vectors between layers provide the simplest method for classifying different repeat patterns.  It is demonstrated that the no. position of honeycomb layers can be directly detd. in STEM-HAADF imaging studies.  Using fault models derived from STEM studies, it is shown that both the sharp, sym. subcell peaks and the broad, asym. superstructure peaks in powder diffraction patterns can be quant. modeled.  About 20% of the layers in the ordered monoclinic sample are faulted in a nonrandom manner, while the disordered sample stacking is not fully random but instead contains about 4% monoclinic order.  Furthermore, it is shown that the ordered sample has a series of higher-order superstructure peaks assocd. with 6-, 9-, 12-, and 15-layer periods whose existence is transiently driven by the presence of long-range strain that is an inherent consequence of the synthesis mechanism revealed through the present diffraction and imaging studies.  This strain is closely assocd. with a monoclinic shear that can be directly calcd. from cell lattice parameters and which is strongly correlated with the degree of ordering in samples.  The present results are broadly applicable to other honeycomb-lattice systems including Li2MnO3 and related Li-excess cathode compns.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref35" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393945"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mueller-Neuhaus, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Understanding factors that influence Li-ion kinetics in pos. electrode materials is important for development of Li-ion batteries capable of operating at high rates and low temps.  Herein, the impact of rate and operating temp. on Li diffusion was studied to explain charge-discharge asymmetry where lithiation is kinetically hindered compared to delithiation.  Co-free, Li-rich Li1.12Ni0.44Mn0.44O2 materials with different secondary particle sizes of 4 and 16μm, but nearly identical primary particle sizes, lattice parameters, and percentage of Ni atoms in the Li layers, were chosen to demonstrate how worsened Li kinetics during lithiation leads to charge-discharge asymmetry.  Cells were cycled between 4.4-1.5 V to eliminate transition metal migration and to understand Li kinetics as a function of state of charge.  Measurements were done on an Ultra-high precision charger to measure apparent capacity loss due to kinetic limitations.  At low and moderate rates, the cell capacity was fully recovered by allowing the cell voltage to pass below 2 V where a voltage plateau corresponding to a Li2MO2-like phase was obsd.  Li ion diffusion measurements confirmed that Li diffusion is slower at low voltage.  Taken together with the rate and temp. dependence, the charge-discharge asymmetry is fully accounted for solely from Li-ion kinetic limitations.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref37" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1016/S0378-7753(01)00638-3"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Van

Mechanochem. degrdn. processes such as the fracture of cathode particles play a major role in limiting the service life of advanced lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  In order to help alleviate the degrdn. of battery performance, it is necessary to measure the relationship between the degrdn. of the mech. properties of cathodes and their concomitant degrdn. of electrochem. performance.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 adopts a layered structure (P2) where the Ni and Mn cations form an ordered arrangement with a √3a × √3a superlattice.  The ordered superlattice is preserved in T2-structure Li2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 prepd. from the Na-contg. parent phase by ion exchange.  In attempts to improve the electrochem. properties of Li2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2, the effect of Co substitution for Ni in the parent phase was studied.  Using x-ray and neutron diffraction, increasing amts. of Co 1st weaken and then suppress the superlattice ordering in the transition metal layers.  For x ≤ 1/12 in Na2/3[CoxNi1/3-xMn2/3]O2 the superlattice persists, but for x ≥ 1/6 the superlattice is not obsd.  During ion exchange to make Li-contg. materials, only samples with well-developed superlattices result in cryst. products; otherwise, a stacking-faulted O2-type structure was obtained.                         cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 cation-disordered electrode for high-rate symmetric rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yuesheng; Xiao, Ruijuan; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Avdeev, Maxim; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most P2-type layered oxides exhibit Na+/vacancy-ordered superstructures because of strong Na+-Na+ interaction in the alkali metal layer and charge ordering in the transition metal layer.  These superstructures evidenced by voltage plateaus in the electrochem. curves limit the Na+ ion transport kinetics and cycle performance in rechargeable batteries.  Here we show that such Na+/vacancy ordering can be avoided by choosing the transition metal ions with similar ionic radii and different redox potentials, for example, Cr3+ and Ti4+.  The designed P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 is completely Na+/vacancy-disordered at any sodium content and displays excellent rate capability and long cycle life.  A sym. sodium-ion battery using the same P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 electrode delivers 75% of the initial capacity at 12C rate.  Our contribution demonstrates that the approach of preventing Na+/vacancy ordering by breaking charge ordering in the transition metal layer opens a simple way to design disordered electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref41" class="refNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit41" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.104535"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schuck, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schumacher, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Preferential Occupation of Na in P3-type Layered Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>70</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">104535</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.104535</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D70%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D104535%26journal%3DNano%2BEnergy%26author%3DL.%2BZhang%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DG.%2BSchuck%26author%3DM.%2BWinter%26author%3DG.%2BSchumacher%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26title%3DPreferential%2BOccupation%2Bof%2BNa%2Bin%2BP3-type%2BLayered%2BCathode%2BMaterial%2Bfor%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.nanoen.2020.104535&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c15654&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.nanoen.2020.104535&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Preferential occupation of Na in P3-type layered cathode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Li; Wang, Jun; Li, Jinke; Schuck, Goetz; Winter, Martin; Schumacher, Gerhard; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">70</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">104535</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered NaxTMO2 (0 &lt; x = 1, TM = transition metal) materials have been extensively investigated as cathodes for sodium ion batteries.  However, capacity fading during cycling hinders their practical utilization in sodium ion batteries.  The capacity fading of layered NaxTMO2 materials is normally reasoned by unfavorable structural phase transitions accompanied by the slide of TMO2 layers, while the shrinkage and expansion of TMO2 layers is still unclear.  P3-type Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33O2, synthesized at 750°C and demonstrating attractive electrochem. behavior, is investigated by X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS).  Exptl. results explain changes in interat. distance and bond angle of TMO6 units during cycling of P3-type material.  The preferential occupancy of Na around Ni in P3-type material is obsd. as a result of an ordered arrangement of Mn and Ni, which is responsible for different dimensional changes of various TMO6 units resulting in stress in the overall structure and capacity fading.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref42" class="refNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit42" data-doi="10.1002/smtd.202000422"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schuck, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xi, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schumacher, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Stabilizing P3-Type Oxides as Cathodes for High-Rate and Long-Life Sodium Ion Batteries by Disordered Distribution of Transition Metals</span>. <i>Small Methods</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2000422</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/smtd.202000422</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D4%26publication_year%3D2020%26pages%3D2000422%26journal%3DSmall%2BMethods%26author%3DL.%2BZhang%26author%3DJ.%2BWang%26author%3DG.%2BSchuck%26author%3DF.%2BXi%26author%3DL.%2BDu%26author%3DM.%2BWinter%26author%3DG.%2BSchumacher%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26title%3DStabilizing%2BP3-Type%2BOxides%2Bas%2BCathodes%2Bfor%2BHigh-Rate%2Band%2BLong-Life%2BSodium%2BIon%2BBatteries%2Bby%2BDisordered%2BDistribution%2Bof%2BTransition%2BMetals%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fsmtd.202000422&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c15654&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fsmtd.202000422&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stabilizing P3-Type Oxides as Cathodes for High-Rate and Long-Life Sodium Ion Batteries by Disordered Distribution of Transition Metals</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Li; Wang, Jun; Schuck, Goetz; Xi, Fanxing; Du, Leilei; Winter, Martin; Schumacher, Gerhard; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small Methods</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000422</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMMECI</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2366-9608</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As potential cathodes for sodium ion batteries, layered NaxTMO2 (0.44 ≤ x ≤ 1, TM = transition metal) materials show high specific capacities but poor cycling and rate performance.  In layered NaxTMO2, the distribution of TM at crystal sites dets. the electrostatic interaction between TM and the coordinated atoms and affects the electrochem. behavior.  Herein, the cycling performance of P3-type Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33O2 materials with ordered and disordered arrangement of TMs is investigated at different rates.  Unlike the TM-ordered material, the disordered one can be cycled at 2000 mA g-1 for 1500 cycles with 89% capacity retention.  X-ray absorption spectroscopy is performed to elucidate the reason for long cycle life of the TM-disordered P3-type Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33O2 from the sight of local structural changes around Mn and Ni.  The exptl. results show that the structural stability of NiO6 units in the TM-disordered material is responsible for its better cycling performance in comparison with that of the TM-ordered material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1149/1.2133112"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weppner, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huggins, R. A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Determination of the Kinetic Parameters of Mixed-Conducting Electrodes and Application to the System Li<sub>3</sub>Sb</span>. <i>J. Electrochem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1977</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>124</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1569</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1578</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/1.2133112</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D124%26publication_year%3D1977%26pages%3D1569-1578%26journal%3DJ.%2BElectrochem.%2BSoc.%26author%3DW.%2BWeppner%26author%3DR.%2BA.%2BHuggins%26title%3DDetermination%2Bof%2Bthe%2BKinetic%2BParameters%2Bof%2BMixed-Conducting%2BElectrodes%2Band%2BApplication%2Bto%2Bthe%2BSystem%2BLi3Sb%26doi%3D10.1149%252F1.2133112&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c15654&amp;doiOfLink=10.1149%2F1.2133112&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit43R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">43</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Determination of the kinetic parameters of mixed-conducting electrodes and application to the system lithium antimonide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Weppner, W.; Huggins, R. A.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1977</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">124</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1569-78</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">An electrochem. galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique is described which combines both transient and steady-state measurements to obtain kinetic properties of solid mixed-conducting electrodes, as well as thermodn. data.  The derivation of quantities such as the chem. and component diffusion coeffs., the partial cond., the mobility, the thermodn. enhancement factor, and the parabolic rate const. as a function of stoichiometry is presented.  A description of the factors governing the equil. of compn. gradients in such phases is included.  The technique is applied to the detn. of the kinetic parameters of Li3Sb, which has a narrow compn. range.  For Li2.9994Sb the chem. diffusion coeff. is 2 × 10-5 cm2-s-1 at 360°.  This value is quite high, due to a large thermodn. enhancement factor of 1.3 × 104.  The Li component diffusion coeff. at this compn. is 1.5 × 10-9 cm2-s3-1. The partial conductivity and elec. mobility of Li are 1.5 × 10-4 Ω-1-cm-1 and 3 × 10-8 cm2-V-1-s-1, resp., at the same stoichiometry and temp.  Because of the very large values of the chem. diffusion coeff. and the fact that 3 mols of Li can react per mol of Sb, this system may be of interest for use in new types of secondary batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref44" class="refNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit44" data-doi="10.1149/1.2128939"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, C.                         cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Thermodynamic and mass transport properties of "lithium aluminide"</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, C. John; Boukamp, B. A.; Huggins, R. A.; Weppner, W.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The compositional variations of the thermodn. and mass transport properties of the β phase "LiAl" in the system were investigated at 415° to 600°.  At 415° the emf. of the single phase "LiAl" lies between 300 and 70 mV relative to pure Li and this corresponds to a Li activity increasing from 0.0063 to 0.31 over the phase stability range from 46.8 to 55.0 at. % Li.  At the ideal stoichiometry, the std. Gibbs free energy of formation of "LiAl" is -29.2 kJ/mol at 415° and the corresponding enthalpy and entropy are -43.3 and -20.6 J/mol K, resp.  Two different electrochem. transient techniques were used to measure the chem. diffusion coeff. in "LiAl" as a function of the stoichiometry; the exptl. results obtained are in good agreement.  On the Li deficient side of ideal stoichiometry, the chem. diffusion coeff. increases with decreasing Li concn., becoming about 10-4 cm2/s near the phase boundary.  On the other hand, it is compn. independent on the Li excess side of "LiAl," varying with temp. from 2.4 × 10-6 cm2/s at 415° to 1.8 × 10-5 cm2/s at 600°.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref45" class="refNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit45" data-doi="10.1107/S0021889811038970"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Momma, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Izumi, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">VESTA 3 for Three-Dimensional Visualization of Crystal, Volumetric and Morphology Data</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C9CS00846B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.                         rightTab-cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-type cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin Ting; Li Huangxu; Zhu Kunjie; Wang Peng-Fei; Liu Pei; Jiao Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society reviews</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temperature sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as promising candidates for smart grids and large-scale energy storage systems (EESs) due to their significant benefits of abundant and low-cost sodium resource.  Among the previously reported cathode materials for SIBs, layered transition-metal oxides and polyanion-type materials are considered to be the most attractive options.  Although many layered transition-metal oxides can provide high capacity due to their small molecular weight, their further application is hindered by low output voltage (mostly lower than 3.5 V), irreversible phase transition as well as storage instability.  Comparatively, polyanion-type materials exhibit higher operating potentials due to the inductive effect of polyanion groups.  Their robust 3D framework significantly decreases the structural variations during sodium ion de/intercalation.  Moreover, the effect of strong X-O (X = S, P, Si, etc.) covalent bonds can effectively inhibit oxygen evolution.  These advantages contribute to the superior cycle stability and high safety of polyanion-type materials.  However, low electronic conductivity and limited capacity still restrict their further application.  This review summarizes the recent progress of polyanion-type materials for SIBs, which include phosphates, fluorophosphates, pyrophosphates, mixed phosphates, sulfates, and silicates.  We also discuss the remaining challenges and corresponding strategies for polyanion-type materials.  We hope this review can provide some insights into the development of polyanionic materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref5" class="rightTabRefNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit5" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-019-08359-y"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mariyappan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rousse, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jacquet, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deschamps, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">David, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mirvaux, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Freeland, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The growing need to store an increasing amt. of renewable energy in a sustainable way has rekindled interest for sodium-ion battery technol., owing to the natural abundance of sodium.  Presently, sodium-ion batteries based on Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C are the subject of intense research focused on improving the energy d. by harnessing the third sodium, which has so far been reported to be electrochem. inaccessible.  Here, we are able to trigger the activity of the third sodium electrochem. via the formation of a disordered NaxV2(PO4)2F3 phase of tetragonal symmetry (I4/mmm space group).  This phase can reversibly uptake 3 sodium ions per formula unit over the 1 to 4.8 V voltage range, with the last one being re-inserted at 1.6 V vs Na+/Na0. With the unprecedentedly increasing demand for renewable and clean energy sources, the sodium-ion battery (SIB) is emerging as an alternative or complementary energy storage candidate to the present com. lithium-ion battery due to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources. By coupling the electrochem. process with an in situ x-ray diffraction expt., the authors identified the succession of single-phase or two-phase domains appearing on Na intercalation with a rather good accuracy compared with previous studies.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Li-substituted layered P2-Na0.80[Li0.12Ni0.22Mn0.66]O2 is studied as an advanced cathode material for Na-ion batteries.  Both n diffraction and NMR spectroscopy are used to elucidate the local structure, and they reveal that most of the Li ions are located in transition metal (TM) sites, preferably surrounded by Mn ions.  To characterize structural changes occurring upon electrochem. cycling, in situ synchrotron x-ray diffraction is conducted.  No significant phase transformation is obsd. up to 4.4 V charge for this material, unlike Li-free P2-type Na cathodes.  The presence of monovalent Li ions in the TM layers allows more Na ions to reside in the prismatic sites, stabilizing the overall charge balance of the compd.  Consequently, more Na ions remain in the compd. upon charge, the P2 structure is retained in the high voltage region, and the phase transformation is delayed.  Ex situ NMR is conducted on samples at different states of charge/discharge to track Li-ion site occupation changes.  Surprisingly, Li is mobile, some Li ions migrate from the TM layer to the Na layer at high voltage, and yet this process is highly reversible.  Novel design principles for Na cathode materials are proposed from an atomistic level understanding of the underlying electrochem. processes.  These principles enable one to devise an optimized, high capacity, and structurally stable compd. as a potential cathode material for high-energy Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1039/C8TA09833F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jo, C. This limitation can be overcome by introducing a sacrificial salt additive, which participates in the electrochem. oxidn. reaction by releasing enough sodium ions to compensate for the insufficient sodium content in the cathode material.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. and structural evolution of monoclinic NaNiO2 as a cathode material for Na-ion battery is reported. Two new phases were discovered: a monoclinic O3 structure (designated as O''''3) at the beginning of the charge (and end of discharge) and a P3 structure (designated as P'''3) at 3.38 V that appeared only during the charge process. We show in this paper that there is a clear relation between the Jahn-Teller activity of a transition metal ion at the end of charge and the mobility of Na in a cathode material.  This is particularly important as mobility at the end of charge limits the capacity of current materials.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Herein, the synthesis of new quaternary layered Na-based oxides of the type Na x Mn y Ni z Fe0.1Mg0.1O2 (0.67≤ x ≤ 1.0; 0.5≤ y ≤ 0.7; 0.1≤ z ≤ 0.3) is described.         </div><div class="casAbstract">O3-type layered sodium transition metal oxides, for example, NaNi0.5Mn0.5-zTizO2, having one sodium per transition metal ion could be attractive pos. electrode materials for achieving high energy d. sodium-ion batteries, provided that we can reversibly utilize their full Na content.  However, the layered structure on cycling undergoes a series of phase transitions in which the fully desodiated O1 phase shows a huge redn. in cell vol. together with cation migration, both of which are detrimental for long-term cycling performance.  Hence, the practical capacity of layered oxides is restricted to solely ∼0.5-0.6 Na (oxidn. up to ∼4 V vs Na+/Na0), avoiding the complete removal of sodium.  Herein, we show that the partial substitution of a redox-active Ni2+ cation by an inactive one (e.g., Zn2+ to form NaNi0.45Zn0.05Mn0.4Ti0.1O2) suppresses the phase transitions at high voltage (&gt;4 V vs Na+/Na0) and helps in utilizing the max. capacity of the material (170 mAh g-1 with ∼0.8 Na) without much degrdns. upon long cycling.  The fully charged phase (Na0.2Ni0.45Zn0.05Mn0.4Ti0.1O2), as detd. by high-resoln. electron transmission microscopy, shows a P3-O1 intergrowth structure in which the O1 phase is present only locally as nanoscale domains.  We believe that the formation of P3-O1 intergrowths in the Zn-substituted material, in contrast to the distinct O1 phase for unsubstituted NaNi0.5Mn0.4Ti0.1O2, restricts structural degrdns. during cycling and improves the long-term cycling stability.  Similar substitution chem. can be extended to Cu2+ and Mg2+ ions as well.  The NaNi0.45Zn0.05Mn0.4Ti0.1O2 pos. electrode material on implementation in 18650 Na-ion cells show electrochem. performances comparable to that of polyanionic Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C cells.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref24" class="rightTabRefNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit24" data-doi="10.1039/C6TA08661F"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bhange, D.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The need to find Na ion battery (SIB) cathodes with high voltage, capacity and improved cycle life has stimulated research on Na contg. layered transition metal oxides.  With this perspective, the electrochem. properties of highly ordered, honeycomb layered Na3Ni2BiO6 with a monoclinic superstructure are explored as a cathode material in SIBs.  It has been demonstrated that Na3Ni2BiO6 delivers a discharge capacity of 106 mAh/g, having high voltage plateaus at 3.50 and 3.25 V, with marginal capacity fading after 50 cycles.  Operando x-ray diffraction studies during charging/discharging reveal 2 reversible 2-phase transition mechanisms (initial O3 phase → P3 intermediate phase → O1 final phase) during sodium extn.  Ex situ x-ray absorption spectroscopy reveals the charge compensation mechanism for the reversible Ni3+/Ni2+ as an active redox couple while Bi5+ being inactive during cycling.  Extended x-ray absorption fine structure anal. shows highly reversible local structural changes around both Ni and Bi atoms occurring during electrochem. cycling.  Unique local structure changes esp. around Ni atoms due to the honeycomb ordering and size mismatch between Ni2+ and Bi5+ are revealed by EXAFS anal. during charging and discharging, which is quite different from the local structure changes in regular layer structured NaMO2 (M =transition metals) cathode materials.  These results suggest that honeycomb layered metal oxides with the general formula, Na3M(II)2M(V)O6, can be considered as candidates for high voltage and long life cathode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref25" class="rightTabRefNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit25" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.7b00930"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-based layered oxides are among the leading cathode candidates for Na-ion batteries, toward potential grid energy storage, having large specific capacity, good ionic cond., and feasible synthesis.  Despite their excellent prospects, the performance of layered intercalation materials is affected by both a phase transition induced by the gliding of the transition metal slabs and air-exposure degrdn. within the Na layers.  Here, this problem is significantly mitigated by selecting 2 ions with very different M-O bond energies to construct a highly ordered Ni6-ring superstructure within the transition metal layers in a model compd. (NaNi2/3Sb1/3O2).  By virtue of substitution of 1/3 Ni with Sb in NaNiO2, the existence of these ordered Ni6-rings with super-exchange interaction to form a sym. at. configuration and degenerate electronic orbital in layered oxides can not only largely enhance their air stability and thermal stability, but also increase the redox potential and simplify the phase-transition process during battery cycling.  The findings reveal that the ordered Ni6-ring superstructure is beneficial for constructing highly stable layered cathodes and calls for new paradigms for better design of layered materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201601698"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries are appealing alternatives to Li-ion battery systems for large-scale energy storage applications in which elemental cost and abundance are important.  Although it is difficult to find Na-ion batteries which achieve substantial specific capacities at voltages above 3 V (vs Na+/Na), the honeycomb-layered compd. Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2 can deliver up to 130 mAh/g of capacity at voltages above 3 V with this capacity concd. in plateaus at 3.27 and 3.64 V.  Comprehensive crystallog. studies have been carried out in order to understand the role of disorder in this system which can be prepd. in both "disordered" and "ordered" forms, depending on the synthesis conditions.  The av. structure of Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2 is always found to adopt an O3-type stacking sequence, though different structures for the disordered (R‾3m, #166, a = b = 3.06253(3) Å and c = 16.05192(7) Å) and ordered variants (C2/m, #12, a = 5.30458(1) Å, b = 9.18432(1) Å, c = 5.62742(1) Å and β = 108.2797(2)°) are demonstrated through the combined Rietveld refinement of synchrotron X-ray and time-of-flight neutron powder diffraction data.  However, pair distribution function studies find that the local structure of disordered Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2 is more correctly described using the honeycomb-ordered structural model, and solid-state NMR studies confirm that the well-developed honeycomb ordering of Ni and Sb cations within the transition-metal layers is indistinguishable from that of the ordered phase.  The disorder is instead found to mainly occur perpendicular to the honeycomb layers with an obsd. coherence length of not much more than 1 nm seen in electron diffraction studies.  When the Na environment is probed through 23Na solid-state NMR, no evidence is found for prismatic Na environments, and a bulk diffraction anal. finds no evidence of conventional stacking faults.  The lack of long-range coherence is instead attributed to disorder among the three possible choices for distributing Ni and Sb cations into a honeycomb lattice in each transition-metal layer, disrupting the Li2MnO3-type stacking of the honeycomb layers preferred in the ordered form of Na(Ni2/3Sb1/3)O2.  It is obsd. that the full theor. discharge capacity expected for a Ni3+/2+ redox couple (133 mAh/g) can be achieved for the ordered variant but not for the disordered variant (∼110 mAh/g). About 20% of the layers in the ordered monoclinic sample are faulted in a nonrandom manner, while the disordered sample stacking is not fully random but instead contains about 4% monoclinic order.
