        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Mechanochem. degrdn. processes such as the fracture of cathode particles play a major role in limiting the service life of advanced lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  In order to help alleviate the degrdn. of battery performance, it is necessary to measure the relationship between the degrdn. of the mech. properties of cathodes and their concomitant degrdn. of electrochem. performance.  In this review, measurements of the mech. properties of LIB cathode materials are summarized from the literature, along with the range of exptl. methods used in their detn.  Dimensional changes that accompany charge and discharge are compared for active materials of olivine, spinel, and layered at. structures.  The sensitivity of indentation hardness, Young's modulus and fracture strength to grain size, porosity, state of charge and charge/discharge history are critically reviewed and discussed with ref. to the behavior of conventional, elec. inactive solids.  This approach allows for the identification of microstructural properties that dictate the mech. properties of LIB cathode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref39"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref39" class="rightTabRefNumLink">39</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit39" data-doi="10.1021/cm000359m"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Donaberger, R. A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahn, J. R.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Superlattice Ordering of Mn, Ni, and Co in Layered Alkali Transition Metal Oxides with P2, P3, and O3 Structures</span>. <i>Chem. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2000</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3583</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3590</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/cm000359m</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit39R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">39</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Superlattice Ordering of Mn, Ni, and Co in Layered Alkali Transition Metal Oxides with P2, P3, and O3 Structures</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Lu, Zhonghua; Donaberger, R. A.; Dahn, J. R.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2000</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">12</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3583-3590</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 adopts a layered structure (P2) where the Ni and Mn cations form an ordered arrangement with a √3a × √3a superlattice.  The ordered superlattice is preserved in T2-structure Li2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 prepd. from the Na-contg. parent phase by ion exchange.  In attempts to improve the electrochem. properties of Li2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2, the effect of Co substitution for Ni in the parent phase was studied.  Using x-ray and neutron diffraction, increasing amts. of Co 1st weaken and then suppress the superlattice ordering in the transition metal layers.  For x ≤ 1/12 in Na2/3[CoxNi1/3-xMn2/3]O2 the superlattice persists, but for x ≥ 1/6 the superlattice is not obsd.  During ion exchange to make Li-contg. materials, only samples with well-developed superlattices result in cryst. products; otherwise, a stacking-faulted O2-type structure was obtained.  The ordering of transition metals in transition metal layers was also found in P3-Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 and O3-Li2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2, but not in O3-Na[Ni1/2Mn1/2]O2.  The necessary conditions for superlattice ordering of transition metal atoms in the transition metal layers are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref40"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref40" class="rightTabRefNumLink">40</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit40" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms7954"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Avdeev, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">P2-Na<sub>0.6</sub>[Cr<sub>0.6</sub>Ti<sub>0.4</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> Cation-Disordered Electrode for High-Rate Symmetric Rechargeable Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">6954</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/ncomms7954</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit40R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">40</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 cation-disordered electrode for high-rate symmetric rechargeable sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Yuesheng; Xiao, Ruijuan; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Avdeev, Maxim; Chen, Liquan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6954</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NCAOBW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-1723</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Most P2-type layered oxides exhibit Na+/vacancy-ordered superstructures because of strong Na+-Na+ interaction in the alkali metal layer and charge ordering in the transition metal layer.  These superstructures evidenced by voltage plateaus in the electrochem. curves limit the Na+ ion transport kinetics and cycle performance in rechargeable batteries.  Here we show that such Na+/vacancy ordering can be avoided by choosing the transition metal ions with similar ionic radii and different redox potentials, for example, Cr3+ and Ti4+.  The designed P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 is completely Na+/vacancy-disordered at any sodium content and displays excellent rate capability and long cycle life.  A sym. sodium-ion battery using the same P2-Na0.6[Cr0.6Ti0.4]O2 electrode delivers 75% of the initial capacity at 12C rate.  Our contribution demonstrates that the approach of preventing Na+/vacancy ordering by breaking charge ordering in the transition metal layer opens a simple way to design disordered electrode materials with high power d. and long cycle life.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref41"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref41" class="rightTabRefNumLink">41</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit41" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.104535"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schuck, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schumacher, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Preferential Occupation of Na in P3-type Layered Cathode Material for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>70</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">104535</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.nanoen.2020.104535</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit41R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">41</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Preferential occupation of Na in P3-type layered cathode material for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Li; Wang, Jun; Li, Jinke; Schuck, Goetz; Winter, Martin; Schumacher, Gerhard; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">70</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">104535</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered NaxTMO2 (0 &lt; x = 1, TM = transition metal) materials have been extensively investigated as cathodes for sodium ion batteries.  However, capacity fading during cycling hinders their practical utilization in sodium ion batteries.  The capacity fading of layered NaxTMO2 materials is normally reasoned by unfavorable structural phase transitions accompanied by the slide of TMO2 layers, while the shrinkage and expansion of TMO2 layers is still unclear.  P3-type Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33O2, synthesized at 750°C and demonstrating attractive electrochem. behavior, is investigated by X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS).  Exptl. results explain changes in interat. distance and bond angle of TMO6 units during cycling of P3-type material.  The preferential occupancy of Na around Ni in P3-type material is obsd. as a result of an ordered arrangement of Mn and Ni, which is responsible for different dimensional changes of various TMO6 units resulting in stress in the overall structure and capacity fading.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref42"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref42" class="rightTabRefNumLink">42</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit42" data-doi="10.1002/smtd.202000422"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schuck, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xi, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Winter, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schumacher, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Stabilizing P3-Type Oxides as Cathodes for High-Rate and Long-Life Sodium Ion Batteries by Disordered Distribution of Transition Metals</span>. <i>Small Methods</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">2000422</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/smtd.202000422</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Stabilizing P3-Type Oxides as Cathodes for High-Rate and Long-Life Sodium Ion Batteries by Disordered Distribution of Transition Metals</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Li; Wang, Jun; Schuck, Goetz; Xi, Fanxing; Du, Leilei; Winter, Martin; Schumacher, Gerhard; Li, Jie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Small Methods</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000422</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">SMMECI</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2366-9608</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">As potential cathodes for sodium ion batteries, layered NaxTMO2 (0.44 ≤ x ≤ 1, TM = transition metal) materials show high specific capacities but poor cycling and rate performance.  In layered NaxTMO2, the distribution of TM at crystal sites dets. the electrostatic interaction between TM and the coordinated atoms and affects the electrochem. behavior.  Herein, the cycling performance of P3-type Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33O2 materials with ordered and disordered arrangement of TMs is investigated at different rates.  Unlike the TM-ordered material, the disordered one can be cycled at 2000 mA g-1 for 1500 cycles with 89% capacity retention.  X-ray absorption spectroscopy is performed to elucidate the reason for long cycle life of the TM-disordered P3-type Na0.67Mn0.67Ni0.33O2 from the sight of local structural changes around Mn and Ni.  The exptl. results show that the structural stability of NiO6 units in the TM-disordered material is responsible for its better cycling performance in comparison with that of the TM-ordered material.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1149/1.2133112"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Weppner, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huggins, R. A.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Determination of the Kinetic Parameters of Mixed-Conducting Electrodes and Application to the System Li<sub>3</sub>Sb</span>. <i>J. Electrochem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1977</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>124</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1569</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1578</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

    </div><div class="casAbstract">An electrochem. galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique is described which combines both transient and steady-state measurements to obtain kinetic properties of solid mixed-conducting electrodes, as well as thermodn. data.  The derivation of quantities such as the chem. and component diffusion coeffs., the partial cond., the mobility, the thermodn. enhancement factor, and the parabolic rate const. as a function of stoichiometry is presented.  A description of the factors governing the equil. of compn. gradients in such phases is included.  The technique is applied to the detn. of the kinetic parameters of Li3Sb, which has a narrow compn. range.  For Li2.9994Sb the chem. diffusion coeff. is 2 × 10-5 cm2-s-1 at 360°.  This value is quite high, due to a large thermodn. enhancement factor of 1.3 × 104.  The Li component diffusion coeff. at this compn. is 1.5 × 10-9 cm2-s3-1. The partial conductivity and elec. mobility of Li are 1.5 × 10-4 Ω-1-cm-1 and 3 × 10-8 cm2-V-1-s-1, resp., at the same stoichiometry and temp.  Because of the very large values of the chem. diffusion coeff. and the fact that 3 mols of Li can react per mol of Sb, this system may be of interest for use in new types of secondary batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref44"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref44" class="rightTabRefNumLink">44</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit44" data-doi="10.1149/1.2128939"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, C.                 10.1149/1.2128939</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Thermodynamic and mass transport properties of "lithium aluminide"</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, C.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The compositional variations of the thermodn. and mass transport properties of the β phase "LiAl" in the system were investigated at 415° to 600°.  At 415° the emf. of the single phase "LiAl" lies between 300 and 70 mV relative to pure Li and this corresponds to a Li activity increasing from 0.0063 to 0.31 over the phase stability range from 46.8 to 55.0 at. % Li.  At the ideal stoichiometry, the std. Gibbs free energy of formation of "LiAl" is -29.2 kJ/mol at 415° and the corresponding enthalpy and entropy are -43.3 and -20.6 J/mol K, resp.  Two different electrochem. transient techniques were used to measure the chem. diffusion coeff. in "LiAl" as a function of the stoichiometry; the exptl. results obtained are in good agreement.  On the Li deficient side of ideal stoichiometry, the chem. diffusion coeff. increases with decreasing Li concn., becoming about 10-4 cm2/s near the phase boundary.  On the other hand, it is compn. independent on the Li excess side of "LiAl," varying with temp. from 2.4 × 10-6 cm2/s at 415° to 1.8 × 10-5 cm2/s at 600°.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref45"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref45" class="rightTabRefNumLink">45</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit45" data-doi="10.1107/S0021889811038970"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Momma, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Izumi, F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">VESTA 3 for Three-Dimensional Visualization of Crystal, Volumetric and Morphology Data</span>. <i>J.         <script nonce="9318825a6cb354b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="9318825a6cb354b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>

<script nonce="9318825a6cb354b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());

        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");

          //Show error message
          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

        <script nonce="9318825a6cb354b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
